I say it is south, for the\r\ncountry is more deserted there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I say east,\xe2\x80\x9d said my patient.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am for west,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the plain-clothes man. \xe2\x80\x9cThere are several\r\nquiet little villages up there.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I am for north,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause there are no hills there, and\r\nour friend says that he did not notice the carriage go up any.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome,\xe2\x80\x9d cried the inspector, laughing; \xe2\x80\x9cit\xe2\x80\x99s a very pretty diversity of\r\nopinion. We have boxed the compass among us. Who do you give your\r\ncasting vote to?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou are all wrong.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut we can\xe2\x80\x99t all be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, you can. This is my point.\xe2\x80\x9d He placed his finger in the\r\ncentre of the circle. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is where we shall find them.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut the twelve-mile drive?\xe2\x80\x9d gasped Hatherley.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSix out and six back. Nothing simpler. You say yourself that the horse\r\nwas fresh and glossy when you got in. How could it be that if it had\r\ngone twelve miles over heavy roads?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed, it is a likely ruse enough,\xe2\x80\x9d observed Bradstreet thoughtfully.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course there can be no doubt as to the nature of this gang.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone at all,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes. \xe2\x80\x9cThey are coiners on a large scale, and\r\nhave used the machine to form the amalgam which has taken the place of\r\nsilver.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe have known for some time that a clever gang was at work,\xe2\x80\x9d said the\r\ninspector. \xe2\x80\x9cThey have been turning out half-crowns by the thousand. We\r\neven traced them as far as Reading, but could get no farther, for they\r\nhad covered their traces in a way that showed that they were very old\r\nhands. But now, thanks to this lucky chance, I think that we have got\r\nthem right enough.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the inspector was mistaken, for those criminals were not destined\r\nto fall into the hands of justice. As we rolled into Eyford Station we\r\nsaw a gigantic column of smoke which streamed up from behind a small\r\nclump of trees in the neighbourhood and hung like an immense ostrich\r\nfeather over the landscape.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA house on fire?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Bradstreet as the train steamed off again on\r\nits way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d said the station-master.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did it break out?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI hear that it was during the night, sir, but it has got worse, and\r\nthe whole place is in a blaze.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhose house is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDr. Becher\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me,\xe2\x80\x9d broke in the engineer, \xe2\x80\x9cis Dr. Becher a German, very thin,\r\nwith a long, sharp nose?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe station-master laughed heartily. \xe2\x80\x9cNo, sir, Dr. Becher is an\r\nEnglishman, and there isn\xe2\x80\x99t a man in the parish who has a better-lined\r\nwaistcoat. But he has a gentleman staying with him, a patient, as I\r\nunderstand, who is a foreigner, and he looks as if a little good\r\nBerkshire beef would do him no harm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe station-master had not finished his speech before we were all\r\nhastening in the direction of the fire. The road topped a low hill, and\r\nthere was a great widespread whitewashed building in front of us,\r\nspouting fire at every chink and window, while in the garden in front\r\nthree fire-engines were vainly striving to keep the flames under.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat\xe2\x80\x99s it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hatherley, in intense excitement. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is the\r\ngravel-drive, and there are the rose-bushes where I lay. That second\r\nwindow is the one that I jumped from.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, at least,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, \xe2\x80\x9cyou have had your revenge upon them.\r\nThere can be no question that it was your oil-lamp which, when it was\r\ncrushed in the press, set fire to the wooden walls, though no doubt\r\nthey were too excited in the chase after you to observe it at the time.\r\nNow keep your eyes open in this crowd for your friends of last night,\r\nthough I very much fear that they are a good hundred miles off by now.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd Holmes\xe2\x80\x99 fears came to be realised, for from that day to this no\r\nword has ever been heard either of the beautiful woman, the sinister\r\nGerman, or the morose Englishman. Early that morning a peasant had met\r\na cart containing several people and some very bulky boxes driving\r\nrapidly in the direction of Reading, but there all traces of the\r\nfugitives disappeared, and even Holmes\xe2\x80\x99 ingenuity failed ever to\r\ndiscover the least clue as to their whereabouts.\r\n\r\nThe firemen had been much perturbed at the strange arrangements which\r\nthey had found within, and still more so by discovering a newly severed\r\nhuman thumb upon a window-sill of the second floor. About sunset,\r\nhowever, their efforts were at last successful, and they subdued the\r\nflames, but not before the roof had fallen in, and the whole place been\r\nreduced to such absolute ruin that, save some twisted cylinders and\r\niron piping, not a trace remained of the machinery which had cost our\r\nunfortunate acquaintance so dearly. Large masses of nickel and of tin\r\nwere discovered stored in an out-house, but no coins were to be found,\r\nwhich may have explained the presence of those bulky boxes which have\r\nbeen already referred to.\r\n\r\nHow our hydraulic engineer had been conveyed from the garden to the\r\nspot where he recovered his senses might have remained forever a\r\nmystery were it not for the soft mould, which told us a very plain\r\ntale. He had evidently been carried down by two persons, one of whom\r\nhad remarkably small feet and the other unusually large ones. On the\r\nwhole, it was most probable that the silent Englishman, being less bold\r\nor less murderous than his companion, had assisted the woman to bear\r\nthe unconscious man out of the way of danger.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell,\xe2\x80\x9d said our engineer ruefully as we took our seats to return once\r\nmore to London, \xe2\x80\x9cit has been a pretty business for me! I have lost my\r\nthumb and I have lost a fifty-guinea fee, and what have I gained?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExperience,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cIndirectly it may be of value,\r\nyou know; you have only to put it into words to gain the reputation of\r\nbeing excellent company for the remainder of your existence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nX. THE ADVENTURE OF THE NOBLE BACHELOR\r\n\r\nThe Lord St. Simon marriage, and its curious termination, have long\r\nceased to be a subject of interest in those exalted circles in which\r\nthe unfortunate bridegroom moves. Fresh scandals have eclipsed it, and\r\ntheir more piquant details have drawn the gossips away from this\r\nfour-year-old drama. As I have reason to believe, however, that the\r\nfull facts have never been revealed to the general public, and as my\r\nfriend Sherlock Holmes had a considerable share in clearing the matter\r\nup, I feel that no memoir of him would be complete without some little\r\nsketch of this remarkable episode.\r\n\r\nIt was a few weeks before my own marriage, during the days when I was\r\nstill sharing rooms with Holmes in Baker Street, that he came home from\r\nan afternoon stroll to find a letter on the table waiting for him. I\r\nhad remained indoors all day, for the weather had taken a sudden turn\r\nto rain, with high autumnal winds, and the jezail bullet which I had\r\nbrought back in one of my limbs as a relic of my Afghan campaign\r\nthrobbed with dull persistence. With my body in one easy-chair and my\r\nlegs upon another, I had surrounded myself with a cloud of newspapers\r\nuntil at last, saturated with the news of the day, I tossed them all\r\naside and lay listless, watching the huge crest and monogram upon the\r\nenvelope upon the table and wondering lazily who my friend\xe2\x80\x99s noble\r\ncorrespondent could be.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is a very fashionable epistle,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked as he entered. \xe2\x80\x9cYour\r\nmorning letters, if I remember right, were from a fish-monger and a\r\ntide-waiter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, my correspondence has certainly the charm of variety,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nanswered, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cand the humbler are usually the more interesting.\r\nThis looks like one of those unwelcome social summonses which call upon\r\na man either to be bored or to lie.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe broke the seal and glanced over the contents.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, come, it may prove to be something of interest, after all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot social, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, distinctly professional.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd from a noble client?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne of the highest in England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear fellow, I congratulate you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI assure you, Watson, without affectation, that the status of my\r\nclient is a matter of less moment to me than the interest of his case.\r\nIt is just possible, however, that that also may not be wanting in this\r\nnew investigation. You have been reading the papers diligently of late,\r\nhave you not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt looks like it,\xe2\x80\x9d said I ruefully, pointing to a huge bundle in the\r\ncorner. \xe2\x80\x9cI have had nothing else to do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is fortunate, for you will perhaps be able to post me up. I read\r\nnothing except the criminal news and the agony column. The latter is\r\nalways instructive. But if you have followed recent events so closely\r\nyou must have read about Lord St. Simon and his wedding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, with the deepest interest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is well. The letter which I hold in my hand is from Lord St.\r\nSimon. I will read it to you, and in return you must turn over these\r\npapers and let me have whatever bears upon the matter. This is what he\r\nsays:\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98MY DEAR MR. SHERLOCK HOLMES,\xe2\x80\x94Lord Backwater tells me that I may\r\n    place implicit reliance upon your judgment and discretion. I have\r\n    determined, therefore, to call upon you and to consult you in\r\n    reference to the very painful event which has occurred in\r\n    connection with my wedding. Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, is\r\n    acting already in the matter, but he assures me that he sees no\r\n    objection to your co-operation, and that he even thinks that it\r\n    might be of some assistance. I will call at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the\r\n    afternoon, and, should you have any other engagement at that time,\r\n    I hope that you will postpone it, as this matter is of paramount\r\n    importance. Yours faithfully,\r\n\r\n\r\n    \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98ROBERT ST. SIMON.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is dated from Grosvenor Mansions, written with a quill pen, and the\r\nnoble lord has had the misfortune to get a smear of ink upon the outer\r\nside of his right little finger,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Holmes as he folded up the\r\nepistle.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe says four o\xe2\x80\x99clock. It is three now. He will be here in an hour.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I have just time, with your assistance, to get clear upon the\r\nsubject. Turn over those papers and arrange the extracts in their order\r\nof time, while I take a glance as to who our client is.\xe2\x80\x9d He picked a\r\nred-covered volume from a line of books of reference beside the\r\nmantelpiece. \xe2\x80\x9cHere he is,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, sitting down and flattening it out\r\nupon his knee. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Lord Robert Walsingham de Vere St. Simon, second son\r\nof the Duke of Balmoral.\xe2\x80\x99 Hum! \xe2\x80\x98Arms: Azure, three caltrops in chief\r\nover a fess sable. Born in 1846.\xe2\x80\x99 He\xe2\x80\x99s forty-one years of age, which is\r\nmature for marriage. Was Under-Secretary for the colonies in a late\r\nadministration. The Duke, his father, was at one time Secretary for\r\nForeign Affairs. They inherit Plantagenet blood by direct descent, and\r\nTudor on the distaff side. Ha! Well, there is nothing very instructive\r\nin all this. I think that I must turn to you Watson, for something more\r\nsolid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have very little difficulty in finding what I want,\xe2\x80\x9d said I, \xe2\x80\x9cfor\r\nthe facts are quite recent, and the matter struck me as remarkable. I\r\nfeared to refer them to you, however, as I knew that you had an inquiry\r\non hand and that you disliked the intrusion of other matters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, you mean the little problem of the Grosvenor Square furniture van.\r\nThat is quite cleared up now\xe2\x80\x94though, indeed, it was obvious from the\r\nfirst. Pray give me the results of your newspaper selections.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere is the first notice which I can find. It is in the personal\r\ncolumn of the _Morning Post_, and dates, as you see, some weeks back:\r\n\xe2\x80\x98A marriage has been arranged,\xe2\x80\x99 it says, \xe2\x80\x98and will, if rumour is\r\ncorrect, very shortly take place, between Lord Robert St. Simon, second\r\nson of the Duke of Balmoral, and Miss Hatty Doran, the only daughter of\r\nAloysius Doran. Esq., of San Francisco, Cal., U.S.A.\xe2\x80\x99 That is all.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTerse and to the point,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Holmes, stretching his long, thin\r\nlegs towards the fire.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere was a paragraph amplifying this in one of the society papers of\r\nthe same week. Ah, here it is: \xe2\x80\x98There will soon be a call for\r\nprotection in the marriage  market, for the present free-trade\r\nprinciple appears to tell heavily against our home product. One by one\r\nthe management of the noble houses of Great Britain is passing into the\r\nhands of our fair cousins from across the Atlantic. An important\r\naddition has been made during the last week to the list of the prizes\r\nwhich have been borne away by these charming invaders. Lord St. Simon,\r\nwho has shown himself for over twenty years proof against the little\r\ngod\xe2\x80\x99s arrows, has now definitely announced his approaching marriage\r\nwith Miss Hatty Doran, the fascinating daughter of a California\r\nmillionaire. Miss Doran, whose graceful figure and striking face\r\nattracted much attention at the Westbury House festivities, is an only\r\nchild, and it is currently reported that her dowry will run to\r\nconsiderably over the six figures, with expectancies for the future. As\r\nit is an open secret that the Duke of Balmoral has been compelled to\r\nsell his pictures within the last few years, and as Lord St. Simon has\r\nno property of his own save the small estate of Birchmoor, it is\r\nobvious that the Californian heiress is not the only gainer by an\r\nalliance which will enable her to make the easy and common transition\r\nfrom a Republican lady to a British peeress.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnything else?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Holmes, yawning.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes; plenty. Then there is another note in the _Morning Post_ to\r\nsay that the marriage would be an absolutely quiet one, that it would\r\nbe at St. George\xe2\x80\x99s, Hanover Square, that only half a dozen intimate\r\nfriends would be invited, and that the party would return to the\r\nfurnished house at Lancaster Gate which has been taken by Mr. Aloysius\r\nDoran. Two days later\xe2\x80\x94that is, on Wednesday last\xe2\x80\x94there is a curt\r\nannouncement that the wedding had taken place, and that the honeymoon\r\nwould be passed at Lord Backwater\xe2\x80\x99s place, near Petersfield. Those are\r\nall the notices which appeared before the disappearance of the bride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBefore the what?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Holmes with a start.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe vanishing of the lady.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did she vanish, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt the wedding breakfast.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed. This is more interesting than it promised to be; quite\r\ndramatic, in fact.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; it struck me as being a little out of the common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey often vanish before the ceremony, and occasionally during the\r\nhoneymoon; but I cannot call to mind anything quite so prompt as this.\r\nPray let me have the details.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI warn you that they are very incomplete.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps we may make them less so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuch as they are, they are set forth in a single article of a morning\r\npaper of yesterday, which I will read to you. It is headed, \xe2\x80\x98Singular\r\nOccurrence at a Fashionable Wedding\xe2\x80\x99:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The family of Lord Robert St. Simon has been thrown into the greatest\r\nconsternation by the strange and painful episodes which have taken\r\nplace in connection with his wedding. The ceremony, as shortly\r\nannounced in the papers of yesterday, occurred on the previous morning;\r\nbut it is only now that it has been possible to confirm the strange\r\nrumours which have been so persistently floating about. In spite of the\r\nattempts of the friends to hush the matter up, so much public attention\r\nhas now been drawn to it that no good purpose can be served by\r\naffecting to disregard what is a common subject for conversation.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The ceremony, which was performed at St. George\xe2\x80\x99s, Hanover Square,\r\nwas a very quiet one, no one being present save the father of the\r\nbride, Mr. Aloysius Doran, the Duchess of Balmoral, Lord Backwater,\r\nLord Eustace and Lady Clara St. Simon (the younger brother and sister\r\nof the bridegroom), and Lady Alicia Whittington. The whole party\r\nproceeded afterwards to the house of Mr. Aloysius Doran, at Lancaster\r\nGate, where breakfast had been prepared. It appears that some little\r\ntrouble was caused by a woman, whose name has not been ascertained, who\r\nendeavoured to force her way into the house after the bridal party,\r\nalleging that she had some claim upon Lord St. Simon. It was only after\r\na painful and prolonged scene that she was ejected by the butler and\r\nthe footman. The bride, who had fortunately entered the house before\r\nthis unpleasant interruption, had sat down to breakfast with the rest,\r\nwhen she complained of a sudden indisposition and retired to her room.\r\nHer prolonged absence having caused some comment, her father followed\r\nher, but learned from her maid that she had only come up to her chamber\r\nfor an instant, caught up an ulster and bonnet, and hurried down to the\r\npassage. One of the footmen declared that he had seen a lady leave the\r\nhouse thus apparelled, but had refused to credit that it was his\r\nmistress, believing her to be with the company. On ascertaining that\r\nhis daughter had disappeared, Mr. Aloysius Doran, in conjunction with\r\nthe bridegroom, instantly put themselves in communication with the\r\npolice, and very energetic inquiries are being made, which will\r\nprobably result in a speedy clearing up of this very singular business.\r\nUp to a late hour last night, however, nothing had transpired as to the\r\nwhereabouts of the missing lady. There are rumours of foul play in the\r\nmatter, and it is said that the police have caused the arrest of the\r\nwoman who had caused the original disturbance, in the belief that, from\r\njealousy or some other motive, she may have been concerned in the\r\nstrange disappearance of the bride.\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd is that all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly one little item in another of the morning papers, but it is a\r\nsuggestive one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd it is\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat Miss Flora Millar, the lady who had caused the disturbance, has\r\nactually been arrested. It appears that she was formerly a _danseuse_\r\nat the Allegro, and that she has known the bridegroom for some years.\r\nThere are no further particulars, and the whole case is in your hands\r\nnow\xe2\x80\x94so far as it has been set forth in the public press.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd an exceedingly interesting case it appears to be. I would not have\r\nmissed it for worlds. But there is a ring at the bell, Watson, and as\r\nthe clock makes it a few minutes after four, I have no doubt that this\r\nwill prove to be our noble client. Do not dream of going, Watson, for I\r\nvery much prefer having a witness, if only as a check to my own\r\nmemory.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord Robert St. Simon,\xe2\x80\x9d announced our page-boy, throwing open the\r\ndoor. A gentleman entered, with a pleasant, cultured face, high-nosed\r\nand pale, with something perhaps of petulance about the mouth, and with\r\nthe steady, well-opened eye of a man whose pleasant lot it had ever\r\nbeen to command and to be obeyed. His manner was brisk, and yet his\r\ngeneral appearance gave an undue impression of age, for he had a slight\r\nforward stoop and a little bend of the knees as he walked. His hair,\r\ntoo, as he swept off his very curly-brimmed hat, was grizzled round the\r\nedges and thin upon the top. As to his dress, it was careful to the\r\nverge of foppishness, with high collar, black frock-coat, white\r\nwaistcoat, yellow gloves, patent-leather shoes, and light-coloured\r\ngaiters. He advanced slowly into the room, turning his head from left\r\nto right, and swinging in his right hand the cord which held his golden\r\neyeglasses.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood-day, Lord St. Simon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, rising and bowing. \xe2\x80\x9cPray take\r\nthe basket-chair. This is my friend and colleague, Dr. Watson. Draw up\r\na little to the fire, and we will talk this matter over.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA most painful matter to me, as you can most readily imagine, Mr.\r\nHolmes. I have been cut to the quick. I understand that you have\r\nalready managed several delicate cases of this sort, sir, though I\r\npresume that they were hardly from the same class of society.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, I am descending.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI beg pardon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy last client of the sort was a king.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, really! I had no idea. And which king?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King of Scandinavia.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! Had he lost his wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou can understand,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes suavely, \xe2\x80\x9cthat I extend to the\r\naffairs of my other clients the same secrecy which I promise to you in\r\nyours.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf course! Very right! very right! I\xe2\x80\x99m sure I beg pardon. As to my own\r\ncase, I am ready to give you any information which may assist you in\r\nforming an opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThank you. I have already learned all that is in the public prints,\r\nnothing more. I presume that I may take it as correct\xe2\x80\x94this article, for\r\nexample, as to the disappearance of the bride.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord St. Simon glanced over it. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, it is correct, as far as it\r\ngoes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut it needs a great deal of supplementing before anyone could offer\r\nan opinion. I think that I may arrive at my facts most directly by\r\nquestioning you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray do so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen did you first meet Miss Hatty Doran?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn San Francisco, a year ago.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were travelling in the States?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid you become engaged then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut you were on a friendly footing?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was amused by her society, and she could see that I was amused.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHer father is very rich?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is said to be the richest man on the Pacific slope.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how did he make his money?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn mining. He had nothing a few years ago. Then he struck gold,\r\ninvested it, and came up by leaps and bounds.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, what is your own impression as to the young lady\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x94your wife\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncharacter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe nobleman swung his glasses a little faster and stared down into the\r\nfire. \xe2\x80\x9cYou see, Mr. Holmes,\xe2\x80\x9d said he, \xe2\x80\x9cmy wife was twenty before her\r\nfather became a rich man. During that time she ran free in a mining\r\ncamp and wandered through woods or mountains, so that her education has\r\ncome from Nature rather than from the schoolmaster. She is what we call\r\nin England a tomboy, with a strong nature, wild and free, unfettered by\r\nany sort of traditions. She is impetuous\xe2\x80\x94volcanic, I was about to say.\r\nShe is swift in making up her mind and fearless in carrying out her\r\nresolutions. On the other hand, I would not have given her the name\r\nwhich I have the honour to bear\xe2\x80\x9d\xe2\x80\x94he gave a little stately cough\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x9chad I\r\nnot thought her to be at bottom a noble woman. I believe that she is\r\ncapable of heroic self-sacrifice and that anything dishonourable would\r\nbe repugnant to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you her photograph?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI brought this with me.\xe2\x80\x9d He opened a locket and showed us the full\r\nface of a very lovely woman. It was not a photograph but an ivory\r\nminiature, and the artist had brought out the full effect of the\r\nlustrous black hair, the large dark eyes, and the exquisite mouth.\r\nHolmes gazed long and earnestly at it. Then he closed the locket and\r\nhanded it back to Lord St. Simon.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe young lady came to London, then, and you renewed your\r\nacquaintance?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, her father brought her over for this last London season. I met\r\nher several times, became engaged to her, and have now married her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe brought, I understand, a considerable dowry?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA fair dowry. Not more than is usual in my family.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd this, of course, remains to you, since the marriage is a _fait\r\naccompli_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI really have made no inquiries on the subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery naturally not. Did you see Miss Doran on the day before the\r\nwedding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWas she in good spirits?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever better. She kept talking of what we should do in our future\r\nlives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed! That is very interesting. And on the morning of the wedding?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was as bright as possible\xe2\x80\x94at least until after the ceremony.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd did you observe any change in her then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, to tell the truth, I saw then the first signs that I had ever\r\nseen that her temper was just a little sharp. The incident however, was\r\ntoo trivial to relate and can have no possible bearing upon the case.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray let us have it, for all that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, it is childish. She dropped her bouquet as we went towards the\r\nvestry. She was passing the front pew at the time, and it fell over\r\ninto the pew. There was a moment\xe2\x80\x99s delay, but the gentleman in the pew\r\nhanded it up to her again, and it did not appear to be the worse for\r\nthe fall. Yet when I spoke to her of the matter, she answered me\r\nabruptly; and in the carriage, on our way home, she seemed absurdly\r\nagitated over this trifling cause.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed! You say that there was a gentleman in the pew. Some of the\r\ngeneral public were present, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes. It is impossible to exclude them when the church is open.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis gentleman was not one of your wife\xe2\x80\x99s friends?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, no; I call him a gentleman by courtesy, but he was quite a\r\ncommon-looking person. I hardly noticed his appearance. But really I\r\nthink that we are wandering rather far from the point.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady St. Simon, then, returned from the wedding in a less cheerful\r\nframe of mind than she had gone to it. What did she do on re-entering\r\nher father\xe2\x80\x99s house?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw her in conversation with her maid.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd who is her maid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlice is her name. She is an American and came from California with\r\nher.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA confidential servant?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA little too much so. It seemed to me that her mistress allowed her to\r\ntake great liberties. Still, of course, in America they look upon these\r\nthings in a different way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow long did she speak to this Alice?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, a few minutes. I had something else to think of.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou did not overhear what they said?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLady St. Simon said something about \xe2\x80\x98jumping a claim.\xe2\x80\x99 She was\r\naccustomed to use slang of the kind. I have no idea what she meant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAmerican slang is very expressive sometimes. And what did your wife do\r\nwhen she finished speaking to her maid?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe walked into the breakfast-room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn your arm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, alone. She was very independent in little matters like that. Then,\r\nafter we had sat down for ten minutes or so, she rose hurriedly,\r\nmuttered some words of apology, and left the room. She never came\r\nback.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut this maid, Alice, as I understand, deposes that she went to her\r\nroom, covered her bride\xe2\x80\x99s dress with a long ulster, put on a bonnet,\r\nand went out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so. And she was afterwards seen walking into Hyde Park in\r\ncompany with Flora Millar, a woman who is now in custody, and who had\r\nalready made a disturbance at Mr. Doran\xe2\x80\x99s house that morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, yes. I should like a few particulars as to this young lady, and\r\nyour relations to her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord St. Simon shrugged his shoulders and raised his eyebrows. \xe2\x80\x9cWe have\r\nbeen on a friendly footing for some years\xe2\x80\x94I may say on a _very_\r\nfriendly footing. She used to be at the Allegro. I have not treated her\r\nungenerously, and she had no just cause of complaint against me, but\r\nyou know what women are, Mr. Holmes. Flora was a dear little thing, but\r\nexceedingly hot-headed and devotedly attached to me. She wrote me\r\ndreadful letters when she heard that I was about to be married, and, to\r\ntell the truth, the reason why I had the marriage celebrated so quietly\r\nwas that I feared lest there might be a scandal in the church. She came\r\nto Mr. Doran\xe2\x80\x99s door just after we returned, and she endeavoured to push\r\nher way in, uttering very abusive expressions towards my wife, and even\r\nthreatening her, but I had foreseen the possibility of something of the\r\nsort, and I had two police fellows there in private clothes, who soon\r\npushed her out again. She was quiet when she saw that there was no good\r\nin making a row.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid your wife hear all this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, thank goodness, she did not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd she was seen walking with this very woman afterwards?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes. That is what Mr. Lestrade, of Scotland Yard, looks upon as so\r\nserious. It is thought that Flora decoyed my wife out and laid some\r\nterrible trap for her.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, it is a possible supposition.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou think so, too?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI did not say a probable one. But you do not yourself look upon this\r\nas likely?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not think Flora would hurt a fly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, jealousy is a strange transformer of characters. Pray what is\r\nyour own theory as to what took place?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, really, I came to seek a theory, not to propound one. I have\r\ngiven you all the facts. Since you ask me, however, I may say that it\r\nhas occurred to me as possible that the excitement of this affair, the\r\nconsciousness that she had made so immense a social stride, had the\r\neffect of causing some little nervous disturbance in my wife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn short, that she had become suddenly deranged?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, really, when I consider that she has turned her back\xe2\x80\x94I will not\r\nsay upon me, but upon so much that many have aspired to without\r\nsuccess\xe2\x80\x94I can hardly explain it in any other fashion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, certainly that is also a conceivable hypothesis,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes,\r\nsmiling. \xe2\x80\x9cAnd now, Lord St. Simon, I think that I have nearly all my\r\ndata. May I ask whether you were seated at the breakfast-table so that\r\nyou could see out of the window?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe could see the other side of the road and the Park.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cQuite so. Then I do not think that I need to detain you longer. I\r\nshall communicate with you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShould you be fortunate enough to solve this problem,\xe2\x80\x9d said our\r\nclient, rising.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have solved it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEh? What was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI say that I have solved it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere, then, is my wife?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat is a detail which I shall speedily supply.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord St. Simon shook his head. \xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid that it will take wiser\r\nheads than yours or mine,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, and bowing in a stately,\r\nold-fashioned manner he departed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is very good of Lord St. Simon to honour my head by putting it on a\r\nlevel with his own,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sherlock Holmes, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cI think that I\r\nshall have a whisky and soda and a cigar after all this\r\ncross-questioning. I had formed my conclusions as to the case before\r\nour client came into the room.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy dear Holmes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have notes of several similar cases, though none, as I remarked\r\nbefore, which were quite as prompt. My whole examination served to turn\r\nmy conjecture into a certainty. Circumstantial evidence is occasionally\r\nvery convincing, as when you find a trout in the milk, to quote\r\nThoreau\xe2\x80\x99s example.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have heard all that you have heard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWithout, however, the knowledge of pre-existing cases which serves me\r\nso well. There was a parallel instance in Aberdeen some years back, and\r\nsomething on very much the same lines at Munich the year after the\r\nFranco-Prussian War. It is one of these cases\xe2\x80\x94but, hullo, here is\r\nLestrade! Good-afternoon, Lestrade! You will find an extra tumbler upon\r\nthe sideboard, and there are cigars in the box.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe official detective was attired in a pea-jacket and cravat, which\r\ngave him a decidedly nautical appearance, and he carried a black canvas\r\nbag in his hand. With a short greeting he seated himself and lit the\r\ncigar which had been offered to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s up, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Holmes with a twinkle in his eye. \xe2\x80\x9cYou look\r\ndissatisfied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd I feel dissatisfied. It is this infernal St. Simon marriage case.\r\nI can make neither head nor tail of the business.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReally! You surprise me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho ever heard of such a mixed affair? Every clue seems to slip\r\nthrough my fingers. I have been at work upon it all day.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd very wet it seems to have made you,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes laying his hand\r\nupon the arm of the pea-jacket.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I have been dragging the Serpentine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn Heaven\xe2\x80\x99s name, what for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn search of the body of Lady St. Simon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSherlock Holmes leaned back in his chair and laughed heartily.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave you dragged the basin of Trafalgar Square fountain?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy? What do you mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBecause you have just as good a chance of finding this lady in the one\r\nas in the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLestrade shot an angry glance at my companion. \xe2\x80\x9cI suppose you know all\r\nabout it,\xe2\x80\x9d he snarled.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWell, I have only just heard the facts, but my mind is made up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed! Then you think that the Serpentine plays no part in the\r\nmatter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think it very unlikely.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen perhaps you will kindly explain how it is that we found this in\r\nit?\xe2\x80\x9d He opened his bag as he spoke, and tumbled onto the floor a\r\nwedding-dress of watered silk, a pair of white satin shoes and a\r\nbride\xe2\x80\x99s wreath and veil, all discoloured and soaked in water. \xe2\x80\x9cThere,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\nsaid he, putting a new wedding-ring upon the top of the pile. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is\r\na little nut for you to crack, Master Holmes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d said my friend, blowing blue rings into the air. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\ndragged them from the Serpentine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. They were found floating near the margin by a park-keeper. They\r\nhave been identified as her clothes, and it seemed to me that if the\r\nclothes were there the body would not be far off.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the same brilliant reasoning, every man\xe2\x80\x99s body is to be found in\r\nthe neighbourhood of his wardrobe. And pray what did you hope to arrive\r\nat through this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt some evidence implicating Flora Millar in the disappearance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am afraid that you will find it difficult.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAre you, indeed, now?\xe2\x80\x9d cried Lestrade with some bitterness. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nafraid, Holmes, that you are not very practical with your deductions\r\nand your inferences. You have made two blunders in as many minutes.\r\nThis dress does implicate Miss Flora Millar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the dress is a pocket. In the pocket is a card-case. In the\r\ncard-case is a note. And here is the very note.\xe2\x80\x9d He slapped it down\r\nupon the table in front of him. \xe2\x80\x9cListen to this: \xe2\x80\x98You will see me when\r\nall is ready. Come at once. F. H. M.\xe2\x80\x99 Now my theory all along has been\r\nthat Lady St. Simon was decoyed away by Flora Millar, and that she,\r\nwith confederates, no doubt, was responsible for her disappearance.\r\nHere, signed with her initials, is the very note which was no doubt\r\nquietly slipped into her hand at the door and which lured her within\r\ntheir reach.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery good, Lestrade,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, laughing. \xe2\x80\x9cYou really are very fine\r\nindeed. Let me see it.\xe2\x80\x9d He took up the paper in a listless way, but his\r\nattention instantly became riveted, and he gave a little cry of\r\nsatisfaction. \xe2\x80\x9cThis is indeed important,\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHa! you find it so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExtremely so. I congratulate you warmly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLestrade rose in his triumph and bent his head to look. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nshrieked, \xe2\x80\x9cyou\xe2\x80\x99re looking at the wrong side!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn the contrary, this is the right side.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe right side? You\xe2\x80\x99re mad! Here is the note written in pencil over\r\nhere.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd over here is what appears to be the fragment of a hotel bill,\r\nwhich interests me deeply.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere\xe2\x80\x99s nothing in it. I looked at it before,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lestrade. \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oct.\r\n4th, rooms 8_s_., breakfast 2_s_. 6_d_., cocktail 1_s_., lunch 2_s_.\r\n6_d_., glass sherry, 8_d_.\xe2\x80\x99 I see nothing in that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVery likely not. It is most important, all the same. As to the note,\r\nit is important also, or at least the initials are, so I congratulate\r\nyou again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI\xe2\x80\x99ve wasted time enough,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lestrade, rising. \xe2\x80\x9cI believe in hard\r\nwork and not in sitting by the fire spinning fine theories. Good-day,\r\nMr. Holmes, and we shall see which gets to the bottom of the matter\r\nfirst.\xe2\x80\x9d He gathered up the garments, thrust them into the bag, and made\r\nfor the door.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust one hint to you, Lestrade,\xe2\x80\x9d drawled Holmes before his rival\r\nvanished; \xe2\x80\x9cI will tell you the true solution of the matter. Lady St.\r\nSimon is a myth. There is not, and there never has been, any such\r\nperson.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLestrade looked sadly at my companion. Then he turned to me, tapped his\r\nforehead three times, shook his head solemnly, and hurried away.\r\n\r\nHe had hardly shut the door behind him when Holmes rose to put on his\r\novercoat. \xe2\x80\x9cThere is something in what the fellow says about outdoor\r\nwork,\xe2\x80\x9d he remarked, \xe2\x80\x9cso I think, Watson, that I must leave you to your\r\npapers for a little.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was after five o\xe2\x80\x99clock when Sherlock Holmes left me, but I had no\r\ntime to be lonely, for within an hour there arrived a confectioner\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nman with a very large flat box. This he unpacked with the help of a\r\nyouth whom he had brought with him, and presently, to my very great\r\nastonishment, a quite epicurean little cold supper began to be laid out\r\nupon our humble lodging-house mahogany. There were a couple of brace of\r\ncold woodcock, a pheasant, a _p\xc3\xa2t\xc3\xa9 de foie gras_ pie with a group of\r\nancient and cobwebby bottles. Having laid out all these luxuries, my\r\ntwo visitors vanished away, like the genii of the Arabian Nights, with\r\nno explanation save that the things had been paid for and were ordered\r\nto this address.\r\n\r\nJust before nine o\xe2\x80\x99clock Sherlock Holmes stepped briskly into the room.\r\nHis features were gravely set, but there was a light in his eye which\r\nmade me think that he had not been disappointed in his conclusions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey have laid the supper, then,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, rubbing his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou seem to expect company. They have laid for five.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, I fancy we may have some company dropping in,\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cI am\r\nsurprised that Lord St. Simon has not already arrived. Ha! I fancy that\r\nI hear his step now upon the stairs.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was indeed our visitor of the afternoon who came bustling in,\r\ndangling his glasses more vigorously than ever, and with a very\r\nperturbed expression upon his aristocratic features.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy messenger reached you, then?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Holmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, and I confess that the contents startled me beyond measure. Have\r\nyou good authority for what you say?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe best possible.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord St. Simon sank into a chair and passed his hand over his forehead.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat will the Duke say,\xe2\x80\x9d he murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cwhen he hears that one of the\r\nfamily has been subjected to such humiliation?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the purest accident. I cannot allow that there is any\r\nhumiliation.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, you look on these things from another standpoint.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI fail to see that anyone is to blame. I can hardly see how the lady\r\ncould have acted otherwise, though her abrupt method of doing it was\r\nundoubtedly to be regretted. Having no mother, she had no one to advise\r\nher at such a crisis.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a slight, sir, a public slight,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord St. Simon, tapping\r\nhis fingers upon the table.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou must make allowance for this poor girl, placed in so unprecedented\r\na position.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will make no allowance. I am very angry indeed, and I have been\r\nshamefully used.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that I heard a ring,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes. \xe2\x80\x9cYes, there are steps on\r\nthe landing. If I cannot persuade you to take a lenient view of the\r\nmatter, Lord St. Simon, I have brought an advocate here who may be more\r\nsuccessful.\xe2\x80\x9d He opened the door and ushered in a lady and gentleman.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLord St. Simon,\xe2\x80\x9d said he \xe2\x80\x9callow me to introduce you to Mr. and Mrs.\r\nFrancis Hay Moulton. The lady, I think, you have already met.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the sight of these newcomers our client had sprung from his seat and\r\nstood very erect, with his eyes cast down and his hand thrust into the\r\nbreast of his frock-coat, a picture of offended dignity. The lady had\r\ntaken a quick step forward and had held out her hand to him, but he\r\nstill refused to raise his eyes. It was as well for his resolution,\r\nperhaps, for her pleading face was one which it was hard to resist.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99re angry, Robert,\xe2\x80\x9d said she. \xe2\x80\x9cWell, I guess you have every cause\r\nto be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray make no apology to me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Lord St. Simon bitterly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, yes, I know that I have treated you real bad and that I should\r\nhave spoken to you before I went; but I was kind of rattled, and from\r\nthe time when I saw Frank here again I just didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what I was\r\ndoing or saying. I only wonder I didn\xe2\x80\x99t fall down and do a faint right\r\nthere before the altar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerhaps, Mrs. Moulton, you would like my friend and me to leave the\r\nroom while you explain this matter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf I may give an opinion,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked the strange gentleman, \xe2\x80\x9cwe\xe2\x80\x99ve had\r\njust a little too much secrecy over this business already. For my part,\r\nI should like all Europe and America to hear the rights of it.\xe2\x80\x9d He was\r\na small, wiry, sunburnt man, clean-shaven, with a sharp face and alert\r\nmanner.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I\xe2\x80\x99ll tell our story right away,\xe2\x80\x9d said the lady. \xe2\x80\x9cFrank here and I\r\nmet in \xe2\x80\x9984, in McQuire\xe2\x80\x99s camp, near the Rockies, where Pa was working a\r\nclaim. We were engaged to each other, Frank and I; but then one day\r\nfather struck a rich pocket and made a pile, while poor Frank here had\r\na claim that petered out and came to nothing. The richer Pa grew the\r\npoorer was Frank; so at last Pa wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t hear of our engagement lasting\r\nany longer, and he took me away to \xe2\x80\x99Frisco. Frank wouldn\xe2\x80\x99t throw up his\r\nhand, though; so he followed me there, and he saw me without Pa knowing\r\nanything about it. It would only have made him mad to know, so we just\r\nfixed it all up for ourselves. Frank said that he would go and make his\r\npile, too, and never come back to claim me until he had as much as Pa.\r\nSo then I promised to wait for him to the end of time and pledged\r\nmyself not to marry anyone else while he lived. \xe2\x80\x98Why shouldn\xe2\x80\x99t we be\r\nmarried right away, then,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98and then I will feel sure of you;\r\nand I won\xe2\x80\x99t claim to be your husband until I come back?\xe2\x80\x99 Well, we\r\ntalked it over, and he had fixed it all up so nicely, with a clergyman\r\nall ready in waiting, that we just did it right there; and then Frank\r\nwent off to seek his fortune, and I went back to Pa.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe next I heard of Frank was that he was in Montana, and then he went\r\nprospecting in Arizona, and then I heard of him from New Mexico. After\r\nthat came a long newspaper story about how a miners\xe2\x80\x99 camp had been\r\nattacked by Apache Indians, and there was my Frank\xe2\x80\x99s name among the\r\nkilled. I fainted dead away, and I was very sick for months after. Pa\r\nthought I had a decline and took me to half the doctors in \xe2\x80\x99Frisco. Not\r\na word of news came for a year and more, so that I never doubted that\r\nFrank was really dead. Then Lord St. Simon came to \xe2\x80\x99Frisco, and we came\r\nto London, and a marriage was arranged, and Pa was very pleased, but I\r\nfelt all the time that no man on this earth would ever take the place\r\nin my heart that had been given to my poor Frank.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStill, if I had married Lord St. Simon, of course I\xe2\x80\x99d have done my\r\nduty by him. We can\xe2\x80\x99t command our love, but we can our actions. I went\r\nto the altar with him with the intention to make him just as good a\r\nwife as it was in me to be. But you may imagine what I felt when, just\r\nas I came to the altar rails, I glanced back and saw Frank standing and\r\nlooking at me out of the first pew. I thought it was his ghost at\r\nfirst; but when I looked again there he was still, with a kind of\r\nquestion in his eyes, as if to ask me whether I were glad or sorry to\r\nsee him. I wonder I didn\xe2\x80\x99t drop. I know that everything was turning\r\nround, and the words of the clergyman were just like the buzz of a bee\r\nin my ear. I didn\xe2\x80\x99t know what to do. Should I stop the service and make\r\na scene in the church? I glanced at him again, and he seemed to know\r\nwhat I was thinking, for he raised his finger to his lips to tell me to\r\nbe still. Then I saw him scribble on a piece of paper, and I knew that\r\nhe was writing me a note. As I passed his pew on the way out I dropped\r\nmy bouquet over to him, and he slipped the note into my hand when he\r\nreturned me the flowers. It was only a line asking me to join him when\r\nhe made the sign to me to do so. Of course I never doubted for a moment\r\nthat my first duty was now to him, and I determined to do just whatever\r\nhe might direct.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen I got back I told my maid, who had known him in California, and\r\nhad always been his friend. I ordered her to say nothing, but to get a\r\nfew things packed and my ulster ready. I know I ought to have spoken to\r\nLord St. Simon, but it was dreadful hard before his mother and all\r\nthose great people. I just made up my mind to run away and explain\r\nafterwards. I hadn\xe2\x80\x99t been at the table ten minutes before I saw Frank\r\nout of the window at the other side of the road. He beckoned to me and\r\nthen began walking into the Park. I slipped out, put on my things, and\r\nfollowed him. Some woman came talking something or other about Lord St.\r\nSimon to me\xe2\x80\x94seemed to me from the little I heard as if he had a little\r\nsecret of his own before marriage also\xe2\x80\x94but I managed to get away from\r\nher and soon overtook Frank. We got into a cab together, and away we\r\ndrove to some lodgings he had taken in Gordon Square, and that was my\r\ntrue wedding after all those years of waiting. Frank had been a\r\nprisoner among the Apaches, had escaped, came on to \xe2\x80\x99Frisco, found that\r\nI had given him up for dead and had gone to England, followed me there,\r\nand had come upon me at last on the very morning of my second wedding.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI saw it in a paper,\xe2\x80\x9d explained the American. \xe2\x80\x9cIt gave the name and\r\nthe church but not where the lady lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen we had a talk as to what we should do, and Frank was all for\r\nopenness, but I was so ashamed of it all that I felt as if I should\r\nlike to vanish away and never see any of them again\xe2\x80\x94just sending a line\r\nto Pa, perhaps, to show him that I was alive. It was awful to me to\r\nthink of all those lords and ladies sitting round that breakfast-table\r\nand waiting for me to come back. So Frank took my wedding-clothes and\r\nthings and made a bundle of them, so that I should not be traced, and\r\ndropped them away somewhere where no one could find them. It is likely\r\nthat we should have gone on to Paris to-morrow, only that this good\r\ngentleman, Mr. Holmes, came round to us this evening, though how he\r\nfound us is more than I can think, and he showed us very clearly and\r\nkindly that I was wrong and that Frank was right, and that we should be\r\nputting ourselves in the wrong if we were so secret. Then he offered to\r\ngive us a chance of talking to Lord St. Simon alone, and so we came\r\nright away round to his rooms at once. Now, Robert, you have heard it\r\nall, and I am very sorry if I have given you pain, and I hope that you\r\ndo not think very meanly of me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord St. Simon had by no means relaxed his rigid attitude, but had\r\nlistened with a frowning brow and a compressed lip to this long\r\nnarrative.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExcuse me,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, \xe2\x80\x9cbut it is not my custom to discuss my most\r\nintimate personal affairs in this public manner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen you won\xe2\x80\x99t forgive me? You won\xe2\x80\x99t shake hands before I go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, certainly, if it would give you any pleasure.\xe2\x80\x9d He put out his hand\r\nand coldly grasped that which she extended to him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had hoped,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Holmes, \xe2\x80\x9cthat you would have joined us in a\r\nfriendly supper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think that there you ask a little too much,\xe2\x80\x9d responded his Lordship.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may be forced to acquiesce in these recent developments, but I can\r\nhardly be expected to make merry over them. I think that with your\r\npermission I will now wish you all a very good-night.\xe2\x80\x9d He included us\r\nall in a sweeping bow and stalked out of the room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen I trust that you at least will honour me with your company,\xe2\x80\x9d said\r\nSherlock Holmes. \xe2\x80\x9cIt is always a joy to meet an American, Mr. Moulton,\r\nfor I am one of those who believe that the folly of a monarch and the\r\nblundering of a minister in far-gone years will not prevent our\r\nchildren from being some day citizens of the same world-wide country\r\nunder a flag which shall be a quartering of the Union Jack with the\r\nStars and Stripes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe case has been an interesting one,\xe2\x80\x9d remarked Holmes when our\r\nvisitors had left us, \xe2\x80\x9cbecause it serves to show very clearly how\r\nsimple the explanation may be of an affair which at first sight seems\r\nto be almost inexplicable. Nothing could be more natural than the\r\nsequence of events as narrated by this lady, and nothing stranger than\r\nthe result when viewed, for instance, by Mr. Lestrade of Scotland\r\nYard.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were not yourself at fault at all, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFrom the first, two facts were very obvious to me, the one that the\r\nlady had been quite willing to undergo the wedding ceremony, the other\r\nthat she had repented of it within a few minutes of returning home.\r\nObviously something had occurred during the morning, then, to cause her\r\nto change her mind. What could that something be? She could not have\r\nspoken to anyone when she was out, for she had been in the company of\r\nthe bridegroom. Had she seen someone, then? If she had, it must be\r\nsomeone from America because she had spent so short a time in this\r\ncountry that she could hardly have allowed anyone to acquire so deep an\r\ninfluence over her that the mere sight of him would induce her to\r\nchange her plans so completely. You see we have already arrived, by a\r\nprocess of exclusion, at the idea that she might have seen an American.\r\nThen who could this American be, and why should he possess so much\r\ninfluence over her? It might be a lover; it might be a husband. Her\r\nyoung womanhood had, I knew, been spent in rough scenes and under\r\nstrange conditions. So far I had got before I ever heard Lord St.\r\nSimon\xe2\x80\x99s narrative. When he told us of a man in a pew, of the change in\r\nthe bride\xe2\x80\x99s manner, of so transparent a device for obtaining a note as\r\nthe dropping of a bouquet, of her resort to her confidential maid, and\r\nof her very significant allusion to claim-jumping\xe2\x80\x94which in miners\xe2\x80\x99\r\nparlance means taking possession of that which another person has a\r\nprior claim to\xe2\x80\x94the whole situation became absolutely clear. She had\r\ngone off with a man, and the man was either a lover or was a previous\r\nhusband\xe2\x80\x94the chances being in favour of the latter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how in the world did you find them?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt might have been difficult, but friend Lestrade held information in\r\nhis hands the value of which he did not himself know. The initials\r\nwere, of course, of the highest importance, but more valuable still was\r\nit to know that within a week he had settled his bill at one of the\r\nmost select London hotels.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow did you deduce the select?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the select prices. Eight shillings for a bed and eightpence for a\r\nglass of sherry pointed to one of the most expensive hotels. There are\r\nnot many in London which charge at that rate. In the second one which I\r\nvisited in Northumberland Avenue, I learned by an inspection of the\r\nbook that Francis H. Moulton, an American gentleman, had left only the\r\nday before, and on looking over the entries against him, I came upon\r\nthe very items which I had seen in the duplicate bill. His letters were\r\nto be forwarded to 226 Gordon Square; so thither I travelled, and being\r\nfortunate enough to find the loving couple at home, I ventured to give\r\nthem some paternal advice and to point out to them that it would be\r\nbetter in every way that they should make their position a little\r\nclearer both to the general public and to Lord St. Simon in particular.\r\nI invited them to meet him here, and, as you see, I made him keep the\r\nappointment.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut with no very good result,\xe2\x80\x9d I remarked. \xe2\x80\x9cHis conduct was certainly\r\nnot very gracious.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, Watson,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, smiling, \xe2\x80\x9cperhaps you would not be very\r\ngracious either, if, after all the trouble of wooing and wedding, you\r\nfound yourself deprived in an instant of wife and of fortune. I think\r\nthat we may judge Lord St. Simon very mercifully and thank our stars\r\nthat we are never likely to find ourselves in the same position. Draw\r\nyour chair up and hand me my violin, for the only problem we have still\r\nto solve is how to while away these bleak autumnal evenings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nXI. THE ADVENTURE OF THE BERYL CORONET\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHolmes,\xe2\x80\x9d said I as I stood one morning in our bow-window looking down\r\nthe street, \xe2\x80\x9chere is a madman coming along. It seems rather sad that\r\nhis relatives should allow him to come out alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMy friend rose lazily from his armchair and stood with his hands in the\r\npockets of his dressing-gown, looking over my shoulder. It was a\r\nbright, crisp February morning, and the snow of the day before still\r\nlay deep upon the ground, shimmering brightly in the wintry sun. Down\r\nthe centre of Baker Street it had been ploughed into a brown crumbly\r\nband by the traffic, but at either side and on the heaped-up edges of\r\nthe footpaths it still lay as white as when it fell. The grey pavement\r\nhad been cleaned and scraped, but was still dangerously slippery, so\r\nthat there were fewer passengers than usual. Indeed, from the direction\r\nof the Metropolitan Station no one was coming save the single gentleman\r\nwhose eccentric conduct had drawn my attention.\r\n\r\nHe was a man of about fifty, tall, portly, and imposing, with a\r\nmassive, strongly marked face and a commanding figure. He was dressed\r\nin a sombre yet rich style, in black frock-coat, shining hat, neat\r\nbrown gaiters, and well-cut pearl-grey trousers. Yet his actions were\r\nin absurd contrast to the dignity of his dress and features, for he was\r\nrunning hard, with occasional little springs, such as a weary man gives\r\nwho is little accustomed to set any tax upon his legs. As he ran he\r\njerked his hands up and down, waggled his head, and writhed his face\r\ninto the most extraordinary contortions.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat on earth can be the matter with him?\xe2\x80\x9d I asked. \xe2\x80\x9cHe is looking up\r\nat the numbers of the houses.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI believe that he is coming here,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, rubbing his hands.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; I rather think he is coming to consult me professionally. I think\r\nthat I recognise the symptoms. Ha! did I not tell you?\xe2\x80\x9d As he spoke,\r\nthe man, puffing and blowing, rushed at our door and pulled at our bell\r\nuntil the whole house resounded with the clanging.\r\n\r\nA few moments later he was in our room, still puffing, still\r\ngesticulating, but with so fixed a look of grief and despair in his\r\neyes that our smiles were turned in an instant to horror and pity. For\r\na while he could not get his words out, but swayed his body and plucked\r\nat his hair like one who has been driven to the extreme limits of his\r\nreason. Then, suddenly springing to his feet, he beat his head against\r\nthe wall with such force that we both rushed upon him and tore him away\r\nto the centre of the room. Sherlock Holmes pushed him down into the\r\neasy-chair and, sitting beside him, patted his hand and chatted with\r\nhim in the easy, soothing tones which he knew so well how to employ.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have come to me to tell your story, have you not?\xe2\x80\x9d said he. \xe2\x80\x9cYou\r\nare fatigued with your haste. Pray wait until you have recovered\r\nyourself, and then I shall be most happy to look into any little\r\nproblem which you may submit to me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man sat for a minute or more with a heaving chest, fighting against\r\nhis emotion. Then he passed his handkerchief over his brow, set his\r\nlips tight, and turned his face towards us.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo doubt you think me mad?\xe2\x80\x9d said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see that you have had some great trouble,\xe2\x80\x9d responded Holmes.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knows I have!\xe2\x80\x94a trouble which is enough to unseat my reason, so\r\nsudden and so terrible is it. Public disgrace I might have faced,\r\nalthough I am a man whose character has never yet borne a stain.\r\nPrivate affliction also is the lot of every man; but the two coming\r\ntogether, and in so frightful a form, have been enough to shake my very\r\nsoul. Besides, it is not I alone. The very noblest in the land may\r\nsuffer unless some way be found out of this horrible affair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPray compose yourself, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said Holmes, \xe2\x80\x9cand let me have a clear\r\naccount of who you are and what it is that has befallen you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy name,\xe2\x80\x9d answered our visitor, \xe2\x80\x9cis probably familiar to your ears. I\r\nam Alexander Holder, of the banking firm of Holder & Stevenson, of\r\nThreadneedle Street.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe name was indeed well known to us as belonging to the senior partner\r\nin the second largest private banking concern in the City of London.\r\nWhat could have happened, then, to bring one of the foremost citizens\r\nof London to this most pitiable pass? We waited, all curiosity, until\r\nwith another effort he braced himself to tell his story.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI feel that time is of value,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9cthat is why I hastened here\r\nwhen the police inspector suggested that I should secure your\r\nco-operation. I came to Baker Street by the Underground and hurried\r\nfrom there on foot, for the cabs go slowly through this snow. That is\r\nwhy I was so out of breath, for I am a man who takes very little\r\nexercise. I feel better now, and I will put the facts before you as\r\nshortly and yet as clearly as I can.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is, of course, well known to you that in a successful banking\r\nbusiness as much depends upon our being able to find remunerative\r\ninvestments for our funds as upon our increasing our connection and the\r\nnumber of our depositors. One of our most lucrative means of laying out\r\nmoney is in the shape of loans, where the security is unimpeachable. We\r\nhave done a good deal in this direction during the last few years, and\r\nthere are many noble families to whom we have advanced large sums upon\r\nthe security of their pictures, libraries, or plate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYesterday morning I was seated in my office at the bank when a card\r\nwas brought in to me by one of the clerks. I started when I saw the\r\nname, for it was that of none other than\xe2\x80\x94well, perhaps even to you I\r\nhad better say no more than that it was a name which is a household\r\nword all over the earth\xe2\x80\x94one of the highest, noblest, most exalted names\r\nin England. I was overwhelmed by the honour and attempted, when he\r\nentered, to say so, but he plunged at once into business with the air\r\nof a man who wishes to hurry quickly through a disagreeable task.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Mr. Holder,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98I have been informed that you are in the habit\r\nof advancing money.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The firm does so when the security is good.\xe2\x80\x99 I answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98It is absolutely essential to me,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98that I should have \xc2\xa3\r\n50,000 at once. I could, of course, borrow so trifling a sum ten times\r\nover from my friends, but I much prefer to make it a matter of business\r\nand to carry out that business myself. In my position you can readily\r\nunderstand that it is unwise to place one\xe2\x80\x99s self under obligations.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98For how long, may I ask, do you want this sum?\xe2\x80\x99 I asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Next Monday I have a large sum due to me, and I shall then most\r\ncertainly repay what you advance, with whatever interest you think it\r\nright to charge. But it is very essential to me that the money should\r\nbe paid at once.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I should be happy to advance it without further parley from my own\r\nprivate purse,\xe2\x80\x99 said I, \xe2\x80\x98were it not that the strain would be rather\r\nmore than it could bear. If, on the other hand, I am to do it in the\r\nname of the firm, then in justice to my partner I must insist that,\r\neven in your case, every businesslike precaution should be taken.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98I should much prefer to have it so,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, raising up a square,\r\nblack morocco case which he had laid beside his chair. \xe2\x80\x98You have\r\ndoubtless heard of the Beryl Coronet?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98One of the most precious public possessions of the empire,\xe2\x80\x99 said I.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Precisely.\xe2\x80\x99 He opened the case, and there, imbedded in soft,\r\nflesh-coloured velvet, lay the magnificent piece of jewellery which he\r\nhad named. \xe2\x80\x98There are thirty-nine enormous beryls,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98and the\r\nprice of the gold chasing is incalculable. The lowest estimate would\r\nput the worth of the coronet at double the sum which I have asked. I am\r\nprepared to leave it with you as my security.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI took the precious case into my hands and looked in some perplexity\r\nfrom it to my illustrious client.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You doubt its value?\xe2\x80\x99 he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Not at all. I only doubt\xe2\x80\x94\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98The propriety of my leaving it. You may set your mind at rest about\r\nthat. I should not dream of doing so were it not absolutely certain\r\nthat I should be able in four days to reclaim it. It is a pure matter\r\nof form. Is the security sufficient?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ample.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You understand, Mr. Holder, that I am giving you a strong proof of\r\nthe confidence which I have in you, founded upon all that I have heard\r\nof you. I rely upon you not only to be discreet and to refrain from all\r\ngossip upon the matter but, above all, to preserve this coronet with\r\nevery possible precaution because I need not say that a great public\r\nscandal would be caused if any harm were to befall it. Any injury to it\r\nwould be almost as serious as its complete loss, for there are no\r\nberyls in the world to match these, and it would be impossible to\r\nreplace them. I leave it with you, however, with every confidence, and\r\nI shall call for it in person on Monday morning.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSeeing that my client was anxious to leave, I said no more but,\r\ncalling for my cashier, I ordered him to pay over fifty \xc2\xa3 1000 notes.\r\nWhen I was alone once more, however, with the precious case lying upon\r\nthe table in front of me, I could not but think with some misgivings of\r\nthe immense responsibility which it entailed upon me. There could be no\r\ndoubt that, as it was a national possession, a horrible scandal would\r\nensue if any misfortune should occur to it. I already regretted having\r\never consented to take charge of it. However, it was too late to alter\r\nthe matter now, so I locked it up in my private safe and turned once\r\nmore to my work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen evening came I felt that it would be an imprudence to leave so\r\nprecious a thing in the office behind me. Bankers\xe2\x80\x99 safes had been\r\nforced before now, and why should not mine be? If so, how terrible\r\nwould be the position in which I should find myself! I determined,\r\ntherefore, that for the next few days I would always carry the case\r\nbackward and forward with me, so that it might never be really out of\r\nmy reach. With this intention, I called a cab and drove out to my house\r\nat Streatham, carrying the jewel with me. I did not breathe freely\r\nuntil I had taken it upstairs and locked it in the bureau of my\r\ndressing-room.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now a word as to my household, Mr. Holmes, for I wish you to\r\nthoroughly understand the situation. My groom and my page sleep out of\r\nthe house, and may be set aside altogether. I have three maid-servants\r\nwho have been with me a number of years and whose absolute reliability\r\nis quite above suspicion. Another, Lucy Parr, the second waiting-maid,\r\nhas only been in my service a few months. She came with an excellent\r\ncharacter, however, and has always given me satisfaction. She is a very\r\npretty girl and has attracted admirers who have occasionally hung about\r\nthe place. That is the only drawback which we have found to her, but we\r\nbelieve her to be a thoroughly good girl in every way.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo much for the servants. My family itself is so small that it will\r\nnot take me long to describe it. I am a widower and have an only son,\r\nArthur. He has been a disappointment to me, Mr. Holmes\xe2\x80\x94a grievous\r\ndisappointment. I have no doubt that I am myself to blame. People tell\r\nme that I have spoiled him. Very likely I have. When my dear wife died\r\nI felt that he was all I had to love. I could not bear to see the smile\r\nfade even for a moment from his face. I have never denied him a wish.\r\nPerhaps it would have been better for both of us had I been sterner,\r\nbut I meant it for the best.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was naturally my intention that he should succeed me in my\r\nbusiness, but he was not of a business turn. He was wild, wayward, and,\r\nto speak the truth, I could not trust him in the handling of large sums\r\nof money. When he was young he became a member of an aristocratic club,\r\nand there, having charming manners, he was soon the intimate of a\r\nnumber of men with long purses and expensive habits. He learned to play\r\nheavily at cards and to squander money on the turf, until he had again\r\nand again to come to me and implore me to give him an advance upon his\r\nallowance, that he might settle his debts of honour. He tried more than\r\nonce to break away from the dangerous company which he was keeping, but\r\neach time the influence of his friend, Sir George Burnwell, was enough\r\nto draw him back again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd, indeed, I could not wonder that such a man as Sir George Burnwell\r\nshould gain an influence over him, for he has frequently brought him to\r\nmy house, and I have found myself that I could hardly resist the\r\nfascination of his manner. He is older than Arthur, a man of the world\r\nto his finger-tips, one who had been everywhere, seen everything, a\r\nbrilliant talker, and a man of great personal beauty. Yet when I think\r\nof him in cold blood, far away from the glamour of his presence, I am\r\nconvinced from his cynical speech and the look which I have caught in\r\nhis eyes that he is one who should be deeply distrusted. So I think,\r\nand so, too, thinks my little Mary, who has a woman\xe2\x80\x99s quick insight\r\ninto character.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd now there is only she to be described. She is my niece; but when\r\nmy brother died five years ago and left her alone in the world I\r\nadopted her, and have looked upon her ever since as my daughter. She is\r\na sunbeam in my house\xe2\x80\x94sweet, loving, beautiful, a wonderful manager and\r\nhousekeeper, yet as tender and quiet and gentle as a woman could be.\r\nShe is my right hand. I do not know what I could do without her. In\r\nonly one matter has she ever gone against my wishes. Twice my boy has\r\nasked her to marry him, for he loves her devotedly, but each time she\r\nhas refused him. I think that if anyone could have drawn him into the\r\nright path it would have been she, and that his marriage might have\r\nchanged his whole life; but now, alas! it is too late\xe2\x80\x94forever too late!\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow, Mr. Holmes, you know the people who live under my roof, and I\r\nshall continue with my miserable story.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen we were taking coffee in the drawing-room that night after\r\ndinner, I told Arthur and Mary my experience, and of the precious\r\ntreasure which we had under our roof, suppressing only the name of my\r\nclient. Lucy Parr, who had brought in the coffee, had, I am sure, left\r\nthe room; but I cannot swear that the door was closed. Mary and Arthur\r\nwere much interested and wished to see the famous coronet, but I\r\nthought it better not to disturb it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Where have you put it?\xe2\x80\x99 asked Arthur.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98In my own bureau.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Well, I hope to goodness the house won\xe2\x80\x99t be burgled during the\r\nnight.\xe2\x80\x99 said he.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98It is locked up,\xe2\x80\x99 I answered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Oh, any old key will fit that bureau. When I was a youngster I have\r\nopened it myself with the key of the box-room cupboard.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe often had a wild way of talking, so that I thought little of what\r\nhe said. He followed me to my room, however, that night with a very\r\ngrave face.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Look here, dad,\xe2\x80\x99 said he with his eyes cast down, \xe2\x80\x98can you let me\r\nhave \xc2\xa3 200?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98No, I cannot!\xe2\x80\x99 I answered sharply. \xe2\x80\x98I have been far too generous with\r\nyou in money matters.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You have been very kind,\xe2\x80\x99 said he, \xe2\x80\x98but I must have this money, or\r\nelse I can never show my face inside the club again.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98And a very good thing, too!\xe2\x80\x99 I cried.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Yes, but you would not have me leave it a dishonoured man,\xe2\x80\x99 said he.\r\n\xe2\x80\x98I could not bear the disgrace. I must raise the money in some way, and\r\nif you will not let me have it, then I must try other means.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was very angry, for this was the third demand during the month. \xe2\x80\x98You\r\nshall not have a farthing from me,\xe2\x80\x99 I cried, on which he bowed and left\r\nthe room without another word.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen he was gone I unlocked my bureau, made sure that my treasure was\r\nsafe, and locked it again. Then I started to go round the house to see\r\nthat all was secure\xe2\x80\x94a duty which I usually leave to Mary but which I\r\nthought it well to perform myself that night. As I came down the stairs\r\nI saw Mary herself at the side window of the hall, which she closed and\r\nfastened as I approached.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tell me, dad,\xe2\x80\x99 said she, looking, I thought, a little disturbed, \xe2\x80\x98did\r\nyou give Lucy, the maid, leave to go out to-night?\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Certainly not.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98She came in just now by the back door. I have no doubt that she has\r\nonly been to the side gate to see someone, but I think that it is\r\nhardly safe and should be stopped.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98You must speak to her in the morning, or I will if you prefer it.
